</style><script type="application/ld+json" data-next-head="">{"@context":"https://schema.org","@type":"BlogPosting","datePublished":"1/4/2022, 5:00:00 PM","description":"When your organization is faced with investigating a security incident, whether that’s something as simple as a phishing campaign or more complex like a determined human adversary, time is of the essence. Collecting and analyzing data are two critical things that need to be performed to quickly get an understanding of the initial scope and impact of the incident. There are several variables around both collecting and analyzing data that can affect the speed at which you might be able to respond. While the method or process of collecting data (or even the availability of relevant incident data) is unique to each organization, the analysis of that data is something that can be sped up to reduce the time it takes to make tactical and strategic decisions.\n&amp;nbsp;\nIn this blog, we’ll show you how Microsoft Incident Response uses the Kusto Query Language (KQL) to quickly analyze data during incident response investigations.\n&amp;nbsp;\nWhy KQL?\nKusto allows for various ingestion methods and various data formats. Data can be structured to best suit your use case in a table using data mappings, and when use cases arise that call for additional data (e.g., third party logs) you can import on the fly via Azure Data Explorer using One-Click Ingestion. In order to query the data, we use and recommend Azure Data Explorer.\n&amp;nbsp;\nData Sources\nWhen Microsoft&amp;nbsp;Incident Response is on the prowl for threat actors in customer environments, we leverage Microsoft Defender for Endpoint and Microsoft Defender for Identity as two primary data sources. Of course, we do not limit our scope to just Microsoft security products. Every organization has its own third-party logs that are vital to the investigation, which typically includes firewalls, VPNs, proxy logs, etc. We take all of this data and ingest it into a Kusto cluster and database to leverage KQL for fast and efficient analysis.\n&amp;nbsp;\nAnalyzing data to scope the attack impact\nThe ultimate outcome of data analysis is to be able to make decisions based on what the data is telling us. Specifically with incident response investigations, data analysis plays a vital role in being able to scope the impact of the attack, identify new leads to hunt down, and provide insight into how to contain the threat. Leaders within the organization need the results of this analysis to quickly understand what they’re facing and to make decisions based on factual data. In the all-too-common cases of a ransomware attack, you may be faced with the decision to minimize the threat by shutting down network access. Do you have enough information to warrant a shutdown of the entire network or are only certain sites affected? Having analysis completed quickly can help in turn allow for faster decision-making.\n&amp;nbsp;\nWhat used to take hours with reviewing logs in Excel or even SQL databases, can now be done in seconds with KQL. This is possible in part due to the power of KQL which comes from native functions that help quickly parse and/or convert data to something more meaningful to an analyst. Some example functionality this provides is being able to decode Base64 data, parse URLs, and even parse command line arguments. Additionally, for any parsing or working with data that requires a bit more complexity than native querying, KQL does have plug-ins that can extend its use by leveraging Python and R.\n&amp;nbsp;\nBuilt-in Functions useful for Incident Response\nNot unlike other large-data or database query languages, KQL allows you to:\n\nfilter your data (with ‘where’ clauses);\npresent your data (with either ‘project’ or ‘render’ clauses); and &amp;nbsp;\naggregate your data (with ‘summarize’ clauses).\n\nThe real power of KQL, though, comes from its various native functions that can be used to parse or transform data on the fly, including (and certainly not limited to) :\n\nbase64_decode_tostring()\ngzip_decompress_from_base64_string()\nipv4_is_private()\nparse_command_line()\nparse_json()\nparse_path()\nparse_url()\nparse_urlquery()\nparse_user_agent()\nparse_xml()\nurl_decode()\nzlib_decompress_from_base64_string()\n\nThese functions are named in a self-explanatory way, but let’s look at a couple use cases that we’ve run across.\n&amp;nbsp;\nDecoding a suspicious PowerShell command\n\n&amp;nbsp;\nSample suspicious PowerShell command decoded via KQL.\n&amp;nbsp;\n&amp;nbsp;\nThe suspicious command referenced here contains a value that is Gzip compressed and Base64 encoded. Here we’re using the built-in function parse_command_line() and gzip_decompress_from_base64_string() to get the plaintext.\n&amp;nbsp;\nParsing a file path using parse_path()\n\n&amp;nbsp;\nSample of parse_path function usage and resulting output.\n&amp;nbsp;\nNotice how in this example we’re able to use parse_path() which returns a dynamic object of the various path components (Scheme, RootPath, DirectoryPath, etc) that can then be queried using either bracket or dot notation.\n&amp;nbsp;\nParsing a suspicious URL using parse_url()\n\n&amp;nbsp;\nSample of parse_url function usage and resulting output.\n&amp;nbsp;\nHere we use parse_url() in order to break down the URL into its various components. The result is a dynamic object that (similar to parse_path() example above), can be queried via bracket or dot notation.\n&amp;nbsp;\nCustom Functions\nAlong with the previously mentioned built-in functions, custom functions can also be created. This allows for the ability to write a query and save it for re-use without having to re-write the query again. Some examples of these can be found on Github for Microsoft 365 Defender Advanced Hunting.\nCustom functions go beyond only being able to surface artifacts of interest. Functions can add context to an artifact. Take the example of a malicious file created on a system:\nC:\\Windows\\temp\\evil.exe\n&amp;nbsp;\nA function can be created to identify what else may have occurred within a set timeframe around the creation of that file. Depending on the data that is collected, this can include other file creations, modifications, event log entries, and more.\n\n&amp;nbsp;\nThe custom function called EventsWithinTimeframe() accepts 3 parameters:\n\nFilepath – in this case, our example from earlier “C:\\Windows\\temp\\evil.exe”\nTimestamp type – for this example we’re focusing on the creation date\nWindow of time – using 3h to indicate 3-hour time window before or after the creation date\n\nThe underlying query of the function takes the CreationDate of the artifact (C:\\Windows\\temp\\evil.exe) and returns all relevant events (event log entries, other file creation events, etc.) that surround the creation time of the artifact +/- 3 hours. Of course, the data you collect may be different and the underlying query will differentiate based on the schema of the data, however, the logic for this example is something that can be implemented to suit your requirements.\n&amp;nbsp;\nCustom functions are limited only by your imagination. These can be simple one-line queries that help surface a particular artifact, a “utility” type function that converts data to a different format, or a more advanced type query that can provide a full context around a given artifact. You can even invoke functions within functions. Thanks to the power and flexibility of KQL the possibilities are endless.\n&amp;nbsp;\nJoining and External Data\nJust like other query languages, KQL can perform a variety of joins. Kusto extends this capability beyond the current database to cross-cluster joins and even allows for external data sources to be queried and referenced. While joining is commonly done when enriching the context of current artifacts of interest (e.g., to another database that contains information about threat actor C2 infrastructure), let’s focus on the external data sources to show how this can be leveraged during incident response engagements, as well.\n&amp;nbsp;\nUsing externaldata() to reference SHA256 hashes\n&amp;nbsp;\n&amp;nbsp;\nSample query using externaldata operator\n&amp;nbsp;\nThis example is taken from a query shared on Microsoft 365 Defender Hunting Queries that checks for SHA256 hashes in an external feed against the SHA256 hashes from mail flow data. What’s great about this is that there is minimal setup to get the external data made useful for enriching the data being analyzed. You can work with a variety of file formats (CSV, JSON, TXT, etc.) and extract the data you need for working with it further.\n&amp;nbsp;\nGoing even further with KQL\nBeyond the functions that are built-in which help during an incident response investigation, KQL allows for even more capability and flexibility by being able to extend its usage with plugins. Incident responders will appreciate the R and Python plugins that Kusto offers to work with data beyond what is possible in native KQL today.\nBoth plugins allow for working with tabular input and will produce tabular output that can be worked with in any follow-on KQL. For the example below, we’ll be focusing on Python.\n&amp;nbsp;\nRecreating PowerShell Script from Event Logs\nIn certain cases, the only remaining artifact that gives the executed PowerShell comes from the PowerShell Operational Event ID 4104 entries, otherwise known as script block logging. In certain cases, the entirety of the PowerShell script is divided into multiple script blocks which must then be merged back together to view the full script. We’ll be using Python to accomplish this.\n&amp;nbsp;\n\n&amp;nbsp;\nLines 1 and 27 are the actual creation of the function. Lines 2 through 10 are used to query our collected data and prepare it for working with Python. Line 11 sets up the output column for our Python script. Line 12 uses the evaluate operator in order to invoke the Python plugin. Lines 13 through 24 are the actual Python that we use to work with the data as a Pandas dataframe. Note that Pandas and numpy are imported by default and other supported Python libraries can be imported as needed.\n&amp;nbsp;\nConclusion\nKQL allows for speed and flexibility when working with large datasets during incident response engagements. The built-in functions that are used to parse various pieces of data allow for analysts to work with what matters to them, rather than spending additional time trying to manually parse and process data. Custom functions provide users a method for taking a query and turning it into a sharable and repeatable action. KQL is further leveraged by enabling users to use scripting languages, such as R and Python, as another way to work with data. Combined, these attributes and functionality, make KQL a highly effective tool for incident responders.\n&amp;nbsp;\n&amp;nbsp;\nThis blog and its contents are subject to the&amp;nbsp;Microsoft Terms of Use.&amp;nbsp; All code and scripts are subject to the applicable terms on Microsoft’s GitHub Repository (e.g., the MIT class="Breadcrumb_lia-crumb-actions__fTD_Z"></div></div></nav><div class="CustomComponent_lia-wrapper__7IqeO" style="--lia-local-bg-color:transparent"><article class="CustomComponent_lia-article__sQ7z4" data-testid="CustomComponent"><section></section></article></div><div class="CustomComponent_lia-wrapper__7IqeO" style="--lia-local-bg-color:transparent"><article class="CustomComponent_lia-article__sQ7z4" data-testid="CustomComponent"><section></section></article></div></header><main id="main-content"><section class="QuiltSection_lia-section-spacing__WK9AB" data-testid="QuiltSection-blog-article"><div class="QuiltSection_lia-section-attached-first__zZ7U_"></div><div data-testid="ThemedContainer" class="ThemedContainer_lia-fixed-container__MPYgN styles_container__6wTQq"><div class="styles_text-center__Pja03 styles_text-break__Ny06M styles_sr-only__NOnjB styles_lia-g-mb-15__YNrGd" data-testid="QuiltSectionHeader"><h2 class="styles_sr-only__NOnjB" data-testid="QuiltSectionHeader.Title">Blog Post</h2></div><div class="styles_row__sjnST"><div style="--lia-content-item-border:none" class="QuiltSection_lia-column__iNmDj styles_col-lg-12__u20eR"><div class="styles_lia-g-section-widget__aHMgk"><div class="BlogArticleWidget_lia-blog-container__PjCkA"><div class="BlogArticleWidget_lia-message-body-container__APZ8H"><div class="MessageViewStandard_lia-wrap__T7pk7"><article class="MessageViewStandard_lia-article__pnxJk styles_lia-g-message-box__arKXm BlogArticleWidget_lia-article__s_eaY" data-testid="StandardMessageView"><section class="MessageViewStandard_lia-section__rh71K" id="uid3044795"><div class="styles_d-flex__7__Y_ styles_flex-column__Vl0ML"><div class="MessageViewStandard_lia-head-wrapper__KIxBz"><div class="MessageViewStandard_lia-title-section__P1a89"><div class="styles_d-flex__7__Y_"><div class="styles_lia-g-subheading__MYxBV MessageViewStandard_lia-node-title__ptFLq NodeTitle_lia-title-and-icon__wEuTM styles_h5__q_74t" data-testid="NodeTitle">Microsoft Security Experts Blog </div><div class="styles_lia-g-subheading__MYxBV MessageViewStandard_lia-node-title__ptFLq styles_lia-g-ml-25__j3n4m">7 MIN READ</div></div><h1 title="Leveraging the Power of KQL in Incident Response" class="styles_text-break__Ny06M MessageViewStandard_lia-cover-image-title__WLVim" data-testid="MessageSubject">Leveraging the Power of KQL in Incident Response</h1><div class="MessageViewStandard_lia-header__m8KYT"><div class="MessageViewStandard_lia-header-main__2zLqy"><div class="styles_lia-g-mr-10__IIyjn"><a data-hovercard-trigger="true" data-testid="userLink" class="styles_d-block__btBet" aria-label="View Profile: Danielle_Veluz" aria-describedby="UserHoverCard-1020973" target="_self" href="/users/danielle_veluz/1020973"><span class="UserAvatar_lia-avatar__HFfcv styles_lia-g-icon-size-50__HExtr" data-testid="userAvatar"><img src="https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/s/gxcuf89792/images/dS0xMDIwOTczLTM1MzU2Nmk0RjZERkFFMjJENTNDQzBD?image-dimensions=50x50" alt="Danielle_Veluz&#x27;s avatar" class="UserAvatar_lia-avatar-img__CUPwZ" aria-hidden="false"/></span></a></div><div class="MessageViewStandard_lia-header-text__6GqFy"><div class="styles_d-flex__7__Y_ styles_align-items-center__OKdH0"><span class="styles_lia-g-divider__X68T2"><a data-hovercard-trigger="true" data-testid="userLink" class="MessageViewStandard_lia-author-text__3ExAu styles_font-weight-bold__3_Kc8 styles_small__seeYj styles_d-flex__7__Y_" aria-label="View Profile: Danielle_Veluz" aria-describedby="UserHoverCard-1020973" target="_self" href="/users/danielle_veluz/1020973">Danielle_Veluz</a></span><div class="styles_lia-g-ml-5__SlnKE MessageViewStandard_lia-author-rank__fonU0" data-testid="userRank"><div class="UserRankLabel_lia-rank__CqPss"><span style="border-color:#333333;color:#333333" class="UserRankLabel_lia-rank-label__epEUI UserRankLabel_lia-is-text__uT_ot"><span class="UserRankLabel_text-break__RX8Tt styles_lia-g-clamp__dg3Sg">Former Employee</span></span></div></div></div><span class="MessageViewStandard_lia-author-post-time__OaTrl" data-testid="messageTime"><span title="January 4, 2022 at 5:00 PM" role="none">Jan 04, 2022</span></span></div></div></div></div></div></div><div class="BlogArticleWidget_lia-blog-content__THh3d"><div class="styles_lia-g-message-body__LkV7_ styles_lia-g-message-body-blog__TUnqH styles_clearfix__xFEoC styles_text-body__F7QRV"><P>When your organization is faced with investigating a security incident, whether that’s something as simple as a phishing campaign or more complex like a determined human adversary, time is of the essence. Collecting and analyzing data are two critical things that need to be performed to quickly get an understanding of the initial scope and impact of the incident. There are several variables around both collecting and analyzing data that can affect the speed at which you might be able to respond. While the method or process of collecting data (or even the availability of relevant incident data) is unique to each organization, the analysis of that data is something that can be sped up to reduce the time it takes to make tactical and strategic decisions.</P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<P>In this blog, we’ll show you how Microsoft Incident Response uses the Kusto Query Language (KQL) to quickly analyze data during incident response investigations.</P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<H2 id="community-3044795-toc-hId-333732086">Why KQL?</H2>
<P>Kusto allows for <A href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/ingest-data-overview" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">various ingestion methods</A> and <A href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/ingestion-supported-formats" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">various data formats</A>. Data can be structured to best suit your use case in a table using <A href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/management/mappings" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">data mappings</A>, and when use cases arise that call for additional data (<EM>e.g</EM>., third party logs) you can import on the fly via Azure Data Explorer using <A href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/ingest-data-one-click" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">One-Click Ingestion</A>. In order to query the data, we use and recommend <A href="https://dataexplorer.azure.com/" target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow noreferrer">Azure Data Explorer</A>.</P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<H3 id="community-3044795-toc-hId-1024293560">Data Sources</H3>
<P>When Microsoft&nbsp;Incident Response is on the prowl for threat actors in customer environments, we leverage Microsoft Defender for Endpoint and Microsoft Defender for Identity as two primary data sources. Of course, we do not limit our scope to just Microsoft security products. Every organization has its own third-party logs that are vital to the investigation, which typically includes firewalls, VPNs, proxy logs, etc. We take all of this data and ingest it into a <A href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/create-cluster-database-portal" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Kusto cluster and database</A> to leverage KQL for fast and efficient analysis.</P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<H3 id="community-3044795-toc-hId--783160903">Analyzing data to scope the attack impact</H3>
<P>The ultimate outcome of data analysis is to be able to make decisions based on what the data is telling us. Specifically with incident response investigations, data analysis plays a vital role in being able to scope the impact of the attack, identify new leads to hunt down, and provide insight into how to contain the threat. Leaders within the organization need the results of this analysis to quickly understand what they’re facing and to make decisions based on factual data. In the all-too-common cases of a ransomware attack, you may be faced with the decision to minimize the threat by shutting down network access. Do you have enough information to warrant a shutdown of the entire network or are only certain sites affected? Having analysis completed quickly can help in turn allow for faster decision-making.</P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<P>What used to take hours with reviewing logs in Excel or even SQL databases, can now be done in seconds with KQL. This is possible in part due to the power of KQL which comes from native functions that help quickly parse and/or convert data to something more meaningful to an analyst. Some example functionality this provides is being able to decode Base64 data, parse URLs, and even parse command line arguments. Additionally, for any parsing or working with data that requires a bit more complexity than native querying, KQL does have plug-ins that can extend its use by leveraging Python and R.</P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<H2 id="community-3044795-toc-hId--793664007">Built-in Functions useful for Incident Response</H2>
<P>Not unlike other large-data or database query languages, KQL allows you to:</P>
<UL>
<LI>filter your data (with ‘where’ clauses);</LI>
<LI>present your data (with either ‘project’ or ‘render’ clauses); and &nbsp;</LI>
<LI>aggregate your data (with ‘summarize’ clauses).</LI>
</UL>
<P>The real power of KQL, though, comes from its various native functions that can be used to parse or transform data on the fly, including (and certainly not limited to) :</P>
<UL>
<LI><A href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/base64_decode_tostringfunction" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">base64_decode_tostring()</A></LI>
<LI><A href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/gzip-base64-decompress" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">gzip_decompress_from_base64_string()</A></LI>
<LI><A href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/ipv4-is-privatefunction" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">ipv4_is_private()</A></LI>
<LI><A href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/parse-command-line" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">parse_command_line()</A></LI>
<LI><A href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/parsejsonfunction" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">parse_json()</A></LI>
<LI><A href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/parsepathfunction" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">parse_path()</A></LI>
<LI><A href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/parseurlfunction" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">parse_url()</A></LI>
<LI><A href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/parseurlqueryfunction" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">parse_urlquery()</A></LI>
<LI><A href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/parse-useragentfunction" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">parse_user_agent()</A></LI>
<LI><A href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/parse-xmlfunction" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">parse_xml()</A></LI>
<LI><A href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/urldecodefunction" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">url_decode()</A></LI>
<LI><A href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/zlib-base64-decompress" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">zlib_decompress_from_base64_string()</A></LI>
</UL>
<P>These functions are named in a self-explanatory way, but let’s look at a couple use cases that we’ve run across.</P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<H3 id="community-3044795-toc-hId--103102533">Decoding a suspicious PowerShell command</H3>
<P><span class="lia-media-object lia-media-is-center lia-media-size-large" data-image-alt="1.png"><button class="lia-media-unstyled-btn" type="button" aria-haspopup="true" aria-label="Enlarge Image 1.png"><img src="https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/s/gxcuf89792/images/bS0zMDQ0Nzk1LTMzNTY4MGk1OEQxM0YyNUNEMzA0RjI2?image-dimensions=999x117&amp;revision=6" width="999" height="117" alt="1.png" /></button></span></P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<P><EM>Sample suspicious PowerShell command decoded via KQL.</EM></P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<P>The suspicious command referenced here contains a value that is Gzip compressed and Base64 encoded. Here we’re using the built-in function parse_command_line() and gzip_decompress_from_base64_string() to get the plaintext.</P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<H3 id="community-3044795-toc-hId--1910556996">Parsing a file path using parse_path()</H3>
<P><span class="lia-media-object lia-media-is-center lia-media-size-large" data-image-alt="2.png"><button class="lia-media-unstyled-btn" type="button" aria-haspopup="true" aria-label="Enlarge Image 2.png"><img src="https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/s/gxcuf89792/images/bS0zMDQ0Nzk1LTMzNTY4MWlCRTdEMEJEMjQwQ0JFMEMw?image-dimensions=999x148&amp;revision=6" width="999" height="148" alt="2.png" /></button></span></P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<P><EM>Sample of parse_path function usage and resulting output.</EM></P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<P>Notice how in this example we’re able to use parse_path() which returns a dynamic object of the various path components (Scheme, RootPath, DirectoryPath, etc) that can then be queried using either bracket or dot notation.</P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<H3 id="community-3044795-toc-hId-576955837">Parsing a suspicious URL using parse_url()</H3>
<P><span class="lia-media-object lia-media-is-center lia-media-size-large" data-image-alt="3-1.png"><button class="lia-media-unstyled-btn" type="button" aria-haspopup="true" aria-label="Enlarge Image 3-1.png"><img src="https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/s/gxcuf89792/images/bS0zMDQ0Nzk1LTMzNTY4NGkzN0IwQTIyRDk4OEQ2MTBD?image-dimensions=999x142&amp;revision=6" width="999" height="142" alt="3-1.png" /></button></span></P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<P><EM>Sample of parse_url function usage and resulting output.</EM></P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<P>Here we use parse_url() in order to break down the URL into its various components. The result is a dynamic object that (similar to parse_path() example above), can be queried via bracket or dot notation.</P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<H3 id="community-3044795-toc-hId--1230498626">Custom Functions</H3>
<P>Along with the previously mentioned built-in functions, custom functions can also be created. This allows for the ability to write a query and save it for re-use without having to re-write the query again. Some examples of these can be found on <A href="https://github.com/microsoft/Microsoft-365-Defender-Hunting-Queries" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Github for Microsoft 365 Defender Advanced Hunting</A>.</P>
<P>Custom functions go beyond only being able to surface artifacts of interest. Functions can add context to an artifact. Take the example of a malicious file created on a system:</P>
<P class="lia-align-center" style="text-align: center;"><FONT size="5"><EM>C:\Windows\temp\evil.exe</EM></FONT></P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<P>A function can be created to identify what else may have occurred within a set timeframe around the creation of that file. Depending on the data that is collected, this can include other file creations, modifications, event log entries, and more.</P>
<P><span class="lia-media-object lia-media-is-center lia-media-size-large" data-image-alt="3.png"><button class="lia-media-unstyled-btn" type="button" aria-haspopup="true" aria-label="Enlarge Image 3.png"><img src="https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/s/gxcuf89792/images/bS0zMDQ0Nzk1LTMzNTY4NWk4NzNDMEU2M0RBQzY5NkM2?image-dimensions=999x41&amp;revision=6" width="999" height="41" alt="3.png" /></button></span></P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<P>The custom function called EventsWithinTimeframe() accepts 3 parameters:</P>
<UL>
<LI>Filepath – in this case, our example from earlier “<EM>C:\Windows\temp\evil.exe”</EM></LI>
<LI>Timestamp type – for this example we’re focusing on the creation date</LI>
<LI>Window of time – using 3h to indicate 3-hour time window before or after the creation date</LI>
</UL>
<P>The underlying query of the function takes the CreationDate of the artifact (C:\Windows\temp\evil.exe) and returns all relevant events (event log entries, other file creation events, etc.) that surround the creation time of the artifact +/- 3 hours. Of course, the data you collect may be different and the underlying query will differentiate based on the schema of the data, however, the logic for this example is something that can be implemented to suit your requirements.</P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<P>Custom functions are limited only by your imagination. These can be simple one-line queries that help surface a particular artifact, a “utility” type function that converts data to a different format, or a more advanced type query that can provide a full context around a given artifact. You can even invoke functions within functions. Thanks to the power and flexibility of KQL the possibilities are endless.</P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<H3 id="community-3044795-toc-hId-1257014207"><STRONG>Joining and External Data</STRONG></H3>
<P>Just like other query languages, KQL can perform a variety of joins. Kusto extends this capability beyond the current database to cross-cluster joins and even allows for external data sources to be queried and referenced. While joining is commonly done when enriching the context of current artifacts of interest (<EM>e.g</EM>., to another database that contains information about threat actor C2 infrastructure), let’s focus on the external data sources to show how this can be leveraged during incident response engagements, as well.</P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<P><EM>Using externaldata() to reference SHA256 hashes</EM></P>
<P>&nbsp;<span class="lia-media-object lia-media-is-center lia-media-size-large" data-image-alt="4.png"><button class="lia-media-unstyled-btn" type="button" aria-haspopup="true" aria-label="Enlarge Image 4.png"><img src="https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/s/gxcuf89792/images/bS0zMDQ0Nzk1LTMzNTY4N2lDNUVFMTMyNEU0QTMwOTY5?image-dimensions=999x185&amp;revision=6" width="999" height="185" alt="4.png" /></button></span></P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<P><EM style="font-family: inherit;">Sample query using externaldata operator</EM></P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<P>This example is taken from a query <A href="https://github.com/microsoft/Microsoft-365-Defender-Hunting-Queries/blob/773ebb498e0aa897678be98c34ffa56359bf29d9/Initial%20access/Check%20for%20Maalware%20Baazar%20(abuse.ch)%20hashes%20in%20your%20mail%20flow.md" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">shared on Microsoft 365 Defender Hunting Queries</A> that checks for SHA256 hashes in an external feed against the SHA256 hashes from mail flow data. What’s great about this is that there is minimal setup to get the external data made useful for enriching the data being analyzed. You can work with a variety of file formats (CSV, JSON, TXT, etc.) and extract the data you need for working with it further.</P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<H2 id="community-3044795-toc-hId-1246511103">Going even further with KQL</H2>
<P>Beyond the functions that are built-in which help during an incident response investigation, KQL allows for even more capability and flexibility by being able to extend its usage with plugins. Incident responders will appreciate the <A href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/rplugin?pivots=azuredataexplorer" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">R</A> and <A href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/pythonplugin?pivots=azuredataexplorer" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">Python</A> plugins that Kusto offers to work with data beyond what is possible in native KQL today.</P>
<P>Both plugins allow for working with tabular input and will produce tabular output that can be worked with in any follow-on KQL. For the example below, we’ll be focusing on Python.</P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<H3 id="community-3044795-toc-hId-1241909098">Recreating PowerShell Script from Event Logs</H3>
<P>In certain cases, the only remaining artifact that gives the executed PowerShell comes from the PowerShell Operational Event ID 4104 entries, otherwise known as script block logging. In certain cases, the entirety of the PowerShell script is divided into multiple script blocks which must then be merged back together to view the full script. We’ll be using Python to accomplish this.</P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<P><span class="lia-media-object lia-media-is-center lia-media-size-large" data-image-alt="5.png"><button class="lia-media-unstyled-btn" type="button" aria-haspopup="true" aria-label="Enlarge Image 5.png"><img src="https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/s/gxcuf89792/images/bS0zMDQ0Nzk1LTMzNTY4OGlENzI3RUVGNURBOTA1ODUy?image-dimensions=999x362&amp;revision=6" width="999" height="362" alt="5.png" /></button></span></P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<P>Lines 1 and 27 are the actual creation of the function. Lines 2 through 10 are used to query our collected data and prepare it for working with Python. Line 11 sets up the output column for our Python script. Line 12 uses the <A href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/evaluateoperator" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">evaluate</A> operator in order to invoke the Python plugin. Lines 13 through 24 are the actual Python that we use to work with the data as a Pandas dataframe. Note that Pandas and numpy are imported by default and other supported Python libraries can be <A href="https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/pythonplugin?pivots=azuredataexplorer#install-packages-for-the-python-plugin" target="_blank" rel="noopener noreferrer">imported as needed</A>.</P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<H2 id="community-3044795-toc-hId--694628084">Conclusion</H2>
<P>KQL allows for speed and flexibility when working with large datasets during incident response engagements. The built-in functions that are used to parse various pieces of data allow for analysts to work with what matters to them, rather than spending additional time trying to manually parse and process data. Custom functions provide users a method for taking a query and turning it into a sharable and repeatable action. KQL is further leveraged by enabling users to use scripting languages, such as R and Python, as another way to work with data. Combined, these attributes and functionality, make KQL a highly effective tool for incident responders.</P>
<P>&nbsp;</P>
<P><EM>&nbsp;</EM></P>

window.location.pathname.replace('/', ''); var rssLink = document.querySelector('[class*=\"social-link-rss\"]'); if(pathSegmentFooter == 'Blogs' || pathSegmentFooter == 'Events'){ const that’s something as simple as a phishing campaign or more complex like a determined human adversary, time is of the essence. Collecting and analyzing data are two critical things that need to be performed to quickly get an understanding of the initial scope and impact of the incident. There are several variables around both collecting and analyzing data that can affect the speed at which you might be able to respond. While the method or process of collecting data (or even the availability of relevant incident data) is unique to each organization, the analysis of that data is something that can be sped up to reduce the time it takes to make tactical and strategic decisions.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eIn this blog, we’ll show you how Microsoft Incident Response uses the Kusto Query Language (KQL) to quickly analyze data during incident response investigations.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH2 id=\"community-3044795-toc-hId-333732086\"\u003eWhy KQL?\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eKusto allows for \u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/ingest-data-overview\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003evarious ingestion methods\u003c/A\u003e and \u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/ingestion-supported-formats\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003evarious data formats\u003c/A\u003e. Data can be structured to best suit your use case in a table using \u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/management/mappings\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003edata mappings\u003c/A\u003e, and when use cases arise that call for additional data (\u003cEM\u003ee.g\u003c/EM\u003e., third party logs) you can import on the fly via Azure Data Explorer using \u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/ingest-data-one-click\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003eOne-Click Ingestion\u003c/A\u003e. In order to query the data, we use and recommend \u003cA href=\"https://dataexplorer.azure.com/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener nofollow noreferrer\"\u003eAzure Data Explorer\u003c/A\u003e.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-3044795-toc-hId-1024293560\"\u003eData Sources\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eWhen Microsoft\u0026nbsp;Incident Response is on the prowl for threat actors in customer environments, we leverage Microsoft Defender for Endpoint and Microsoft Defender for Identity as two primary data sources. Of course, we do not limit our scope to just Microsoft security products. Every organization has its own third-party logs that are vital to the investigation, which typically includes firewalls, VPNs, proxy logs, etc. We take all of this data and ingest it into a \u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/create-cluster-database-portal\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003eKusto cluster and database\u003c/A\u003e to leverage KQL for fast and efficient analysis.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-3044795-toc-hId--783160903\"\u003eAnalyzing data to scope the attack impact\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eThe ultimate outcome of data analysis is to be able to make decisions based on what the data is telling us. Specifically with incident response investigations, data analysis plays a vital role in being able to scope the impact of the attack, identify new leads to hunt down, and provide insight into how to contain the threat. Leaders within the organization need the results of this analysis to quickly understand what they’re facing and to make decisions based on factual data. In the all-too-common cases of a ransomware attack, you may be faced with the decision to minimize the threat by shutting down network access. Do you have enough information to warrant a shutdown of the entire network or are only certain sites affected? Having analysis completed quickly can help in turn allow for faster decision-making.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eWhat used to take hours with reviewing logs in Excel or even SQL databases, can now be done in seconds with KQL. This is possible in part due to the power of KQL which comes from native functions that help quickly parse and/or convert data to something more meaningful to an analyst. Some example functionality this provides is being able to decode Base64 data, parse URLs, and even parse command line arguments. Additionally, for any parsing or working with data that requires a bit more complexity than native querying, KQL does have plug-ins that can extend its use by leveraging Python and R.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH2 id=\"community-3044795-toc-hId--793664007\"\u003eBuilt-in Functions useful for Incident Response\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eNot unlike other large-data or database query languages, KQL allows you to:\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003efilter your data (with ‘where’ clauses);\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003epresent your data (with either ‘project’ or ‘render’ clauses); and \u0026nbsp;\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eaggregate your data (with ‘summarize’ clauses).\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eThe real power of KQL, though, comes from its various native functions that can be used to parse or transform data on the fly, including (and certainly not limited to) :\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/base64_decode_tostringfunction\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003ebase64_decode_tostring()\u003c/A\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/gzip-base64-decompress\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003egzip_decompress_from_base64_string()\u003c/A\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/ipv4-is-privatefunction\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003eipv4_is_private()\u003c/A\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/parse-command-line\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003eparse_command_line()\u003c/A\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/parsejsonfunction\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003eparse_json()\u003c/A\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/parsepathfunction\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003eparse_url()\u003c/A\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/parseurlqueryfunction\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003eparse_urlquery()\u003c/A\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/parse-useragentfunction\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003eparse_user_agent()\u003c/A\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/parse-xmlfunction\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003eparse_xml()\u003c/A\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/urldecodefunction\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003eurl_decode()\u003c/A\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/zlib-base64-decompress\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003ezlib_decompress_from_base64_string()\u003c/A\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eThese functions are named in a self-explanatory way, but let’s look at a couple use cases that we’ve run across.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-3044795-toc-hId--103102533\"\u003eDecoding a suspicious PowerShell command\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cspan class=\"lia-media-object lia-media-is-center lia-media-size-large\" data-image-alt=\"1.png\"\u003e\u003cbutton class=\"lia-media-unstyled-btn\" type=\"button\" aria-haspopup=\"true\" aria-label=\"Enlarge Image 1.png\"\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/s/gxcuf89792/images/bS0zMDQ0Nzk1LTMzNTY4MGk1OEQxM0YyNUNEMzA0RjI2?image-dimensions=999x117\u0026amp;revision=6\" width=\"999\" height=\"117\" alt=\"1.png\" /\u003e\u003c/button\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cEM\u003eSample suspicious PowerShell command decoded via KQL.\u003c/EM\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eThe suspicious command referenced here contains a value that is Gzip compressed and Base64 encoded. Here we’re using the built-in function parse_command_line() and gzip_decompress_from_base64_string() to get the plaintext.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-3044795-toc-hId--1910556996\"\u003eParsing a file path using parse_path()\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cspan class=\"lia-media-object lia-media-is-center lia-media-size-large\" data-image-alt=\"2.png\"\u003e\u003cbutton class=\"lia-media-unstyled-btn\" type=\"button\" aria-haspopup=\"true\" aria-label=\"Enlarge Image 2.png\"\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/s/gxcuf89792/images/bS0zMDQ0Nzk1LTMzNTY4MWlCRTdEMEJEMjQwQ0JFMEMw?image-dimensions=999x148\u0026amp;revision=6\" width=\"999\" height=\"148\" alt=\"2.png\" /\u003e\u003c/button\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cEM\u003eSample of parse_path function usage and resulting output.\u003c/EM\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eNotice how in this example we’re able to use parse_path() which returns a dynamic object of the various path components (Scheme, RootPath, DirectoryPath, etc) that can then be queried using either bracket or dot notation.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-3044795-toc-hId-576955837\"\u003eParsing a suspicious URL using parse_url()\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cspan class=\"lia-media-object lia-media-is-center lia-media-size-large\" data-image-alt=\"3-1.png\"\u003e\u003cbutton class=\"lia-media-unstyled-btn\" type=\"button\" aria-haspopup=\"true\" aria-label=\"Enlarge Image 3-1.png\"\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/s/gxcuf89792/images/bS0zMDQ0Nzk1LTMzNTY4NGkzN0IwQTIyRDk4OEQ2MTBD?image-dimensions=999x142\u0026amp;revision=6\" width=\"999\" height=\"142\" alt=\"3-1.png\" /\u003e\u003c/button\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cEM\u003eSample of parse_url function usage and resulting output.\u003c/EM\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eHere we use parse_url() in order to break down the URL into its various components. The result is a dynamic object that (similar to parse_path() example above), can be queried via bracket or dot notation.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-3044795-toc-hId--1230498626\"\u003eCustom Functions\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eAlong with the previously mentioned built-in functions, custom functions can also be created. This allows for the ability to write a query and save it for re-use without having to re-write the query again. Some examples of these can be found on \u003cA href=\"https://github.com/microsoft/Microsoft-365-Defender-Hunting-Queries\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003eGithub for Microsoft 365 Defender Advanced Hunting\u003c/A\u003e.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eCustom functions go beyond only being able to surface artifacts of interest. Functions can add context to an artifact. Take the example of a malicious file created on a system:\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP class=\"lia-align-center\" style=\"text-align: center;\"\u003e\u003cFONT size=\"5\"\u003e\u003cEM\u003eC:\\Windows\\temp\\evil.exe\u003c/EM\u003e\u003c/FONT\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eA function can be created to identify what else may have occurred within a set timeframe around the creation of that file. Depending on the data that is collected, this can include other file creations, modifications, event log entries, and more.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cspan class=\"lia-media-object lia-media-is-center lia-media-size-large\" data-image-alt=\"3.png\"\u003e\u003cbutton class=\"lia-media-unstyled-btn\" type=\"button\" aria-haspopup=\"true\" aria-label=\"Enlarge Image 3.png\"\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/s/gxcuf89792/images/bS0zMDQ0Nzk1LTMzNTY4NWk4NzNDMEU2M0RBQzY5NkM2?image-dimensions=999x41\u0026amp;revision=6\" width=\"999\" height=\"41\" alt=\"3.png\" /\u003e\u003c/button\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eThe custom function called EventsWithinTimeframe() accepts 3 parameters:\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eFilepath – in this case, our example from earlier “\u003cEM\u003eC:\\Windows\\temp\\evil.exe”\u003c/EM\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eTimestamp type – for this example we’re focusing on the creation date\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eWindow of time – using 3h to indicate 3-hour time window before or after the creation date\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eThe underlying query of the function takes the CreationDate of the artifact (C:\\Windows\\temp\\evil.exe) and returns all relevant events (event log entries, other file creation events, etc.) that surround the creation time of the artifact +/- 3 hours. Of course, the data you collect may be different and the underlying query will differentiate based on the schema of the data, however, the logic for this example is something that can be implemented to suit your requirements.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eCustom functions are limited only by your imagination. These can be simple one-line queries that help surface a particular artifact, a “utility” type function that converts data to a different format, or a more advanced type query that can provide a full context around a given artifact. You can even invoke functions within functions. Thanks to the power and flexibility of KQL the possibilities are endless.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-3044795-toc-hId-1257014207\"\u003e\u003cSTRONG\u003eJoining and External Data\u003c/STRONG\u003e\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eJust like other query languages, KQL can perform a variety of joins. Kusto extends this capability beyond the current database to cross-cluster joins and even allows for external data sources to be queried and referenced. While joining is commonly done when enriching the context of current artifacts of interest (\u003cEM\u003ee.g\u003c/EM\u003e., to another database that contains information about threat actor C2 infrastructure), let’s focus on the external data sources to show how this can be leveraged during incident response engagements, as well.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cEM\u003eUsing externaldata() to reference SHA256 hashes\u003c/EM\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003cspan class=\"lia-media-object lia-media-is-center lia-media-size-large\" data-image-alt=\"4.png\"\u003e\u003cbutton class=\"lia-media-unstyled-btn\" type=\"button\" aria-haspopup=\"true\" aria-label=\"Enlarge Image 4.png\"\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/s/gxcuf89792/images/bS0zMDQ0Nzk1LTMzNTY4N2lDNUVFMTMyNEU0QTMwOTY5?image-dimensions=999x185\u0026amp;revision=6\" width=\"999\" height=\"185\" alt=\"4.png\" /\u003e\u003c/button\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cEM style=\"font-family: inherit;\"\u003eSample query using externaldata operator\u003c/EM\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eThis example is taken from a query \u003cA href=\"https://github.com/microsoft/Microsoft-365-Defender-Hunting-Queries/blob/773ebb498e0aa897678be98c34ffa56359bf29d9/Initial%20access/Check%20for%20Maalware%20Baazar%20(abuse.ch)%20hashes%20in%20your%20mail%20flow.md\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003eshared on Microsoft 365 Defender Hunting Queries\u003c/A\u003e that checks for SHA256 hashes in an external feed against the SHA256 hashes from mail flow data. What’s great about this is that there is minimal setup to get the external data made useful for enriching the data being analyzed. You can work with a variety of file formats (CSV, JSON, TXT, etc.) and extract the data you need for working with it further.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH2 id=\"community-3044795-toc-hId-1246511103\"\u003eGoing even further with KQL\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eBeyond the functions that are built-in which help during an incident response investigation, KQL allows for even more capability and flexibility by being able to extend its usage with plugins. Incident responders will appreciate the \u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/rplugin?pivots=azuredataexplorer\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003eR\u003c/A\u003e and \u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/pythonplugin?pivots=azuredataexplorer\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003ePython\u003c/A\u003e plugins that Kusto offers to work with data beyond what is possible in native KQL today.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eBoth plugins allow for working with tabular input and will produce tabular output that can be worked with in any follow-on KQL. For the example below, we’ll be focusing on Python.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH3 id=\"community-3044795-toc-hId-1241909098\"\u003eRecreating PowerShell Script from Event Logs\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eIn certain cases, the only remaining artifact that gives the executed PowerShell comes from the PowerShell Operational Event ID 4104 entries, otherwise known as script block logging. In certain cases, the entirety of the PowerShell script is divided into multiple script blocks which must then be merged back together to view the full script. We’ll be using Python to accomplish this.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cspan class=\"lia-media-object lia-media-is-center lia-media-size-large\" data-image-alt=\"5.png\"\u003e\u003cbutton class=\"lia-media-unstyled-btn\" type=\"button\" aria-haspopup=\"true\" aria-label=\"Enlarge Image 5.png\"\u003e\u003cimg src=\"https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/s/gxcuf89792/images/bS0zMDQ0Nzk1LTMzNTY4OGlENzI3RUVGNURBOTA1ODUy?image-dimensions=999x362\u0026amp;revision=6\" width=\"999\" height=\"362\" alt=\"5.png\" /\u003e\u003c/button\u003e\u003c/span\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eLines 1 and 27 are the actual creation of the function. Lines 2 through 10 are used to query our collected data and prepare it for working with Python. Line 11 sets up the output column for our Python script. Line 12 uses the \u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/evaluateoperator\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003eevaluate\u003c/A\u003e operator in order to invoke the Python plugin. Lines 13 through 24 are the actual Python that we use to work with the data as a Pandas dataframe. Note that Pandas and numpy are imported by default and other supported Python libraries can be \u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/pythonplugin?pivots=azuredataexplorer#install-packages-for-the-python-plugin\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003eimported as needed\u003c/A\u003e.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH2 id=\"community-3044795-toc-hId--694628084\"\u003eConclusion\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eKQL allows for speed and flexibility when working with large datasets during incident response engagements. The built-in functions that are used to parse various pieces of data allow for analysts to work with what matters to them, rather than spending additional time trying to manually parse and process data. Custom functions provide users a method for taking a query and turning it into a sharable and repeatable action. KQL is further leveraged by enabling users to use scripting languages, such as R and Python, as another way to work with data. Combined, these attributes and functionality, make KQL a highly effective tool for incident responders.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cEM\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/EM\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cEM\u003eThis blog and its contents are subject to the\u0026nbsp;\u003c/EM\u003e\u003cA href=\"https://www.microsoft.com/en-us/legal/intellectualproperty/copyright\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener noreferrer\"\u003e\u003cEM\u003eMicrosoft Terms of Use\u003c/EM\u003e\u003c/A\u003e\u003cEM\u003e.\u0026nbsp; All code and scripts are subject to the applicable terms on Microsoft’s GitHub Repository (e.g., the MIT License).\u003c/EM\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e","body@stringLength":"18383","rawBody":"\u003cP\u003eWhen your organization is faced with investigating a security incident, whether that’s something as simple as a phishing campaign or more complex like a determined human adversary, time is of the essence. Collecting and analyzing data are two critical things that need to be performed to quickly get an understanding of the initial scope and impact of the incident. There are several variables around both collecting and analyzing data that can affect the speed at which you might be able to respond. While the method or process of collecting data (or even the availability of relevant incident data) is unique to each organization, the analysis of that data is something that can be sped up to reduce the time it takes to make tactical and strategic decisions.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eIn this blog, we’ll show you how Microsoft Incident Response uses the Kusto Query Language (KQL) to quickly analyze data during incident response investigations.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH2\u003eWhy KQL?\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eKusto allows for \u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/ingest-data-overview\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003evarious ingestion methods\u003c/A\u003e and \u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/ingestion-supported-formats\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003evarious data formats\u003c/A\u003e. Data can be structured to best suit your use case in a table using \u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/management/mappings\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003edata mappings\u003c/A\u003e, and when use cases arise that call for additional data (\u003cEM\u003ee.g\u003c/EM\u003e., third party logs) you can import on the fly via Azure Data Explorer using \u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/ingest-data-one-click\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eOne-Click Ingestion\u003c/A\u003e. In order to query the data, we use and recommend \u003cA href=\"https://dataexplorer.azure.com/\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eAzure Data Explorer\u003c/A\u003e.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003eData Sources\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eWhen Microsoft\u0026nbsp;Incident Response is on the prowl for threat actors in customer environments, we leverage Microsoft Defender for Endpoint and Microsoft Defender for Identity as two primary data sources. Of course, we do not limit our scope to just Microsoft security products. Every organization has its own third-party logs that are vital to the investigation, which typically includes firewalls, VPNs, proxy logs, etc. We take all of this data and ingest it into a \u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/create-cluster-database-portal\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eKusto cluster and database\u003c/A\u003e to leverage KQL for fast and efficient analysis.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003eAnalyzing data to scope the attack impact\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eThe ultimate outcome of data analysis is to be able to make decisions based on what the data is telling us. Specifically with incident response investigations, data analysis plays a vital role in being able to scope the impact of the attack, identify new leads to hunt down, and provide insight into how to contain the threat. Leaders within the organization need the results of this analysis to quickly understand what they’re facing and to make decisions based on factual data. In the all-too-common cases of a ransomware attack, you may be faced with the decision to minimize the threat by shutting down network access. Do you have enough information to warrant a shutdown of the entire network or are only certain sites affected? Having analysis completed quickly can help in turn allow for faster decision-making.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eWhat used to take hours with reviewing logs in Excel or even SQL databases, can now be done in seconds with KQL. This is possible in part due to the power of KQL which comes from native functions that help quickly parse and/or convert data to something more meaningful to an analyst. Some example functionality this provides is being able to decode Base64 data, parse URLs, and even parse command line arguments. Additionally, for any parsing or working with data that requires a bit more complexity than native querying, KQL does have plug-ins that can extend its use by leveraging Python and R.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH2\u003eBuilt-in Functions useful for Incident Response\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eNot unlike other large-data or database query languages, KQL allows you to:\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003efilter your data (with ‘where’ clauses);\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003epresent your data (with either ‘project’ or ‘render’ clauses); and \u0026nbsp;\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eaggregate your data (with ‘summarize’ clauses).\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eThe real power of KQL, though, comes from its various native functions that can be used to parse or transform data on the fly, including (and certainly not limited to) :\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/base64_decode_tostringfunction\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003ebase64_decode_tostring()\u003c/A\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/gzip-base64-decompress\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003egzip_decompress_from_base64_string()\u003c/A\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cA rel=\"noopener\"\u003eipv4_is_private()\u003c/A\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/parse-command-line\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eparse_command_line()\u003c/A\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/parsejsonfunction\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eparse_json()\u003c/A\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/parsepathfunction\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eparse_path()\u003c/A\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/parseurlfunction\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eparse_url()\u003c/A\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/parseurlqueryfunction\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eparse_urlquery()\u003c/A\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/parse-useragentfunction\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eparse_user_agent()\u003c/A\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/parse-xmlfunction\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eparse_xml()\u003c/A\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/urldecodefunction\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eurl_decode()\u003c/A\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003e\u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/zlib-base64-decompress\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003ezlib_decompress_from_base64_string()\u003c/A\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eThese functions are named in a self-explanatory way, but let’s look at a couple use cases that we’ve run across.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003eDecoding a suspicious PowerShell command\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cLI-IMAGE width=\"999\" height=\"117\" alt=\"1.png\" align=\"center\" id=\"335680i58D13F25CD304F26\" size=\"large\" sourcetype=\"new\" src=\"https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/s/gxcuf89792/images/bS0zMDQ0Nzk1LTMzNTY4MGk1OEQxM0YyNUNEMzA0RjI2?image-dimensions=750x750\u0026amp;revision=6\"\u003e\u003c/LI-IMAGE\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cEM\u003eSample suspicious PowerShell command decoded via KQL.\u003c/EM\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eThe suspicious command referenced here contains a value that is Gzip compressed and Base64 encoded. Here we’re using the built-in function parse_command_line() and gzip_decompress_from_base64_string() to get the plaintext.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003eParsing a file path using parse_path()\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cLI-IMAGE width=\"999\" height=\"148\" alt=\"2.png\" align=\"center\" id=\"335681iBE7D0BD240CBE0C0\" size=\"large\" sourcetype=\"new\" src=\"https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/s/gxcuf89792/images/bS0zMDQ0Nzk1LTMzNTY4MWlCRTdEMEJEMjQwQ0JFMEMw?image-dimensions=750x750\u0026amp;revision=6\"\u003e\u003c/LI-IMAGE\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cEM\u003eSample of parse_path function usage and resulting output.\u003c/EM\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eNotice how in this example we’re able to use parse_path() which returns a dynamic object of the various path components (Scheme, RootPath, DirectoryPath, etc) that can then be queried using either bracket or dot notation.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003eParsing a suspicious URL using parse_url()\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cLI-IMAGE width=\"999\" height=\"142\" alt=\"3-1.png\" align=\"center\" id=\"335684i37B0A22D988D610C\" size=\"large\" sourcetype=\"new\" src=\"https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/s/gxcuf89792/images/bS0zMDQ0Nzk1LTMzNTY4NGkzN0IwQTIyRDk4OEQ2MTBD?image-dimensions=750x750\u0026amp;revision=6\"\u003e\u003c/LI-IMAGE\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cEM\u003eSample of parse_url function usage and resulting output.\u003c/EM\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eHere we use parse_url() in order to break down the URL into its various components. The result is a dynamic object that (similar to parse_path() example above), can be queried via bracket or dot notation.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003eCustom Functions\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eAlong with the previously mentioned built-in functions, custom functions can also be created. This allows for the ability to write a query and save it for re-use without having to re-write the query again. Some examples of these can be found on \u003cA href=\"https://github.com/microsoft/Microsoft-365-Defender-Hunting-Queries\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eGithub for Microsoft 365 Defender Advanced Hunting\u003c/A\u003e.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eCustom functions go beyond only being able to surface artifacts of interest. Functions can add context to an artifact. Take the example of a malicious file created on a system:\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP class=\"lia-align-center\"\u003e\u003cFONT size=\"5\"\u003e\u003cEM\u003eC:\\Windows\\temp\\evil.exe\u003c/EM\u003e\u003c/FONT\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eA function can be created to identify what else may have occurred within a set timeframe around the creation of that file. Depending on the data that is collected, this can include other file creations, modifications, event log entries, and more.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cLI-IMAGE width=\"999\" height=\"41\" alt=\"3.png\" align=\"center\" id=\"335685i873C0E63DAC696C6\" size=\"large\" sourcetype=\"new\" src=\"https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/s/gxcuf89792/images/bS0zMDQ0Nzk1LTMzNTY4NWk4NzNDMEU2M0RBQzY5NkM2?image-dimensions=750x750\u0026amp;revision=6\"\u003e\u003c/LI-IMAGE\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eThe custom function called EventsWithinTimeframe() accepts 3 parameters:\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cUL\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eFilepath – in this case, our example from earlier “\u003cEM\u003eC:\\Windows\\temp\\evil.exe”\u003c/EM\u003e\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eTimestamp type – for this example we’re focusing on the creation date\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003cLI\u003eWindow of time – using 3h to indicate 3-hour time window before or after the creation date\u003c/LI\u003e\n\u003c/UL\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eThe underlying query of the function takes the CreationDate of the artifact (C:\\Windows\\temp\\evil.exe) and returns all relevant events (event log entries, other file creation events, etc.) that surround the creation time of the artifact +/- 3 hours. Of course, the data you collect may be different and the underlying query will differentiate based on the schema of the data, however, the logic for this example is something that can be implemented to suit your requirements.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eCustom functions are limited only by your imagination. These can be simple one-line queries that help surface a particular artifact, a “utility” type function that converts data to a different format, or a more advanced type query that can provide a full context around a given artifact. You can even invoke functions within functions. Thanks to the power and flexibility of KQL the possibilities are endless.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003e\u003cSTRONG\u003eJoining and External Data\u003c/STRONG\u003e\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eJust like other query languages, KQL can perform a variety of joins. Kusto extends this capability beyond the current database to cross-cluster joins and even allows for external data sources to be queried and referenced. While joining is commonly done when enriching the context of current artifacts of interest (\u003cEM\u003ee.g\u003c/EM\u003e., to another database that contains information about threat actor C2 infrastructure), let’s focus on the external data sources to show how this can be leveraged during incident response engagements, as well.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cEM\u003eUsing externaldata() to reference SHA256 hashes\u003c/EM\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003cLI-IMAGE width=\"999\" height=\"185\" alt=\"4.png\" align=\"center\" id=\"335687iC5EE1324E4A30969\" size=\"large\" sourcetype=\"new\" src=\"https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/s/gxcuf89792/images/bS0zMDQ0Nzk1LTMzNTY4N2lDNUVFMTMyNEU0QTMwOTY5?image-dimensions=750x750\u0026amp;revision=6\"\u003e\u003c/LI-IMAGE\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cEM style=\"font-family: inherit;\"\u003eSample query using externaldata operator\u003c/EM\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eThis example is taken from a query \u003cA href=\"https://github.com/microsoft/Microsoft-365-Defender-Hunting-Queries/blob/773ebb498e0aa897678be98c34ffa56359bf29d9/Initial%20access/Check%20for%20Maalware%20Baazar%20(abuse.ch)%20hashes%20in%20your%20mail%20flow.md\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eshared on Microsoft 365 Defender Hunting Queries\u003c/A\u003e that checks for SHA256 hashes in an external feed against the SHA256 hashes from mail flow data. What’s great about this is that there is minimal setup to get the external data made useful for enriching the data being analyzed. You can work with a variety of file formats (CSV, JSON, TXT, etc.) and extract the data you need for working with it further.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH2\u003eGoing even further with KQL\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eBeyond the functions that are built-in which help during an incident response investigation, KQL allows for even more capability and flexibility by being able to extend its usage with plugins. Incident responders will appreciate the \u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/rplugin?pivots=azuredataexplorer\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eR\u003c/A\u003e and \u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/pythonplugin?pivots=azuredataexplorer\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003ePython\u003c/A\u003e plugins that Kusto offers to work with data beyond what is possible in native KQL today.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eBoth plugins allow for working with tabular input and will produce tabular output that can be worked with in any follow-on KQL. For the example below, we’ll be focusing on Python.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH3\u003eRecreating PowerShell Script from Event Logs\u003c/H3\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eIn certain cases, the only remaining artifact that gives the executed PowerShell comes from the PowerShell Operational Event ID 4104 entries, otherwise known as script block logging. In certain cases, the entirety of the PowerShell script is divided into multiple script blocks which must then be merged back together to view the full script. We’ll be using Python to accomplish this.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cLI-IMAGE width=\"999\" height=\"362\" alt=\"5.png\" align=\"center\" id=\"335688iD727EEF5DA905852\" size=\"large\" sourcetype=\"new\" src=\"https://techcommunity.microsoft.com/t5/s/gxcuf89792/images/bS0zMDQ0Nzk1LTMzNTY4OGlENzI3RUVGNURBOTA1ODUy?image-dimensions=750x750\u0026amp;revision=6\"\u003e\u003c/LI-IMAGE\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eLines 1 and 27 are the actual creation of the function. Lines 2 through 10 are used to query our collected data and prepare it for working with Python. Line 11 sets up the output column for our Python script. Line 12 uses the \u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/evaluateoperator\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eevaluate\u003c/A\u003e operator in order to invoke the Python plugin. Lines 13 through 24 are the actual Python that we use to work with the data as a Pandas dataframe. Note that Pandas and numpy are imported by default and other supported Python libraries can be \u003cA href=\"https://docs.microsoft.com/en-us/azure/data-explorer/kusto/query/pythonplugin?pivots=azuredataexplorer#install-packages-for-the-python-plugin\" target=\"_blank\" rel=\"noopener\"\u003eimported as needed\u003c/A\u003e.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cH2\u003eConclusion\u003c/H2\u003e\n\u003cP\u003eKQL allows for speed and flexibility when working with large datasets during incident response engagements. The built-in functions that are used to parse various pieces of data allow for analysts to work with what matters to them, rather than spending additional time trying to manually parse and process data. Custom functions provide users a method for taking a query and turning it into a sharable and repeatable action. KQL is further leveraged by enabling users to use scripting languages, such as R and Python, as another way to work with data. Combined, these attributes and functionality, make KQL a highly effective tool for incident responders.\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cEM\u003e\u0026nbsp;\u003c/EM\u003e\u003c/P\u003e\n\u003cP\u003e\u003cEM\u003eThis blog and its contents are
